<p><b>Computer security</b>, also known as <b>cybersecurity</b> or <b><a href="/wiki/Information_technology" title="Information technology">IT</a> security</b>, is the protection of <a href="/wiki/Information_system" title="Information system">information systems</a> from theft or damage to the <a href="/wiki/Computer_hardware" title="Computer hardware">hardware</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a>, and to the <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a> on them, as well as from <a href="/wiki/Denial-of-service_attack" title="Denial-of-service attack">disruption</a> or <a href="/wiki/Botnet" title="Botnet">misdirection</a> of the services they provide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>It includes <a href="/wiki/Physical_security" title="Physical security">controlling physical access</a> to the hardware, as well as protecting against harm that may come via <a href="/wiki/Computer_network" title="Computer network">network access</a>, <a href="/wiki/SQL_injection" title="SQL injection">data</a> and <a href="/wiki/Code_injection" title="Code injection">code injection</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and due to malpractice by operators, whether <a href="/wiki/Insider_threat" title="Insider threat">intentional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Error-tolerant_design" title="Error-tolerant design">accidental</a>, or due to them <a href="/wiki/Social_engineering_(security)" title="Social engineering (security)">being tricked</a> into deviating from secure procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The field is of growing importance due to the increasing reliance on computer systems and the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> in most societies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wireless_network" title="Wireless network">wireless networks</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Bluetooth" title="Bluetooth">Bluetooth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> - and the growth of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smart_devices" title="Smart devices">"smart" devices</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">televisions</a> and tiny devices as part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internet_of_Things" title="Internet of Things">Internet of Things</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-59"><a href="#Job_market"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Job market</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-60"><a href="#Terminology"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Terminology</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-61"><a href="#Scholars"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Scholars</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-62"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-63"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-64"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-65"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">19</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vulnerabilities_and_attacks">Vulnerabilities and attacks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Vulnerabilities and attacks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_(computing)" title="Vulnerability (computing)">Vulnerability (computing)</a></div>
<p>A vulnerability is a system susceptibility or flaw. Many vulnerabilities are documented in the <a href="/wiki/Common_Vulnerabilities_and_Exposures" title="Common Vulnerabilities and Exposures">Common Vulnerabilities and Exposures</a> (CVE) database. An <i>exploitable</i> vulnerability is one for which at least one working attack or "<a href="/wiki/Exploit_(computer_security)" title="Exploit (computer security)">exploit"</a> exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>To secure a computer system, it is important to understand the attacks that can be made against it, and these <a href="/wiki/Threat_(computer)" title="Threat (computer)">threats</a> can typically be classified into one of the categories below:</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Backdoors">Backdoors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Backdoors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Backdoor_(computing)" title="Backdoor (computing)">backdoor</a> in a computer system, a <a href="/wiki/Cryptosystem" title="Cryptosystem">cryptosystem</a> or an algorithm, is any secret method of bypassing normal authentication or security controls. They may exist for a number of reasons, including by original design or from poor configuration. They may have been added by an authorized party to allow some legitimate access, or by an attacker for malicious reasons; but regardless of the motives for their existence, they create a vulnerability.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Denial-of-service_attack">Denial-of-service attack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Denial-of-service attack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denial_of_service_attacks" title="Denial of service attacks">Denial of service attacks</a> are designed to make a machine or network resource unavailable to its intended users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Attackers can deny service to individual victims, such as by deliberately entering a wrong password enough consecutive times to cause the victim account to be locked, or they may overload the capabilities of a machine or network and block all users at once. While a network attack from a single IP address can be blocked by adding a new firewall rule, many forms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distributed_denial_of_service#Distributed_attack" title="Distributed denial of service">Distributed denial of service</a> (DDoS) attacks are possible, where the attack comes from a large number of points – and defending is much more difficult. Such attacks can originate from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zombie_computer" title="Zombie computer">zombie computers</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Botnet" title="Botnet">botnet</a>, but a range of other techniques are possible including <a href="/wiki/Denial-of-service_attack#Reflected_.2F_spoofed_attack" title="Denial-of-service attack">reflection and amplification attacks</a>, where innocent systems are fooled into sending traffic to the victim.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct-access_attacks">Direct-access attacks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Direct-access attacks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An unauthorized user gaining physical access to a computer is most likely able to directly copy data from it. They may also compromise security by making <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a> modifications, installing software <a href="/wiki/Computer_worm" title="Computer worm">worms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Keystroke_logging" title="Keystroke logging">keyloggers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Covert_listening_device" title="Covert listening device">covert listening devices</a> or using wireless mice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Even when the system is protected by standard security measures, these may be able to be by-passed by booting another operating system or tool from a <a href="/wiki/CD-ROM" title="CD-ROM">CD-ROM</a> or other bootable media. <a href="/wiki/Disk_encryption" title="Disk encryption">Disk encryption</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trusted_Platform_Module" title="Trusted Platform Module">Trusted Platform Module</a> are designed to prevent these attacks.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eavesdropping">Eavesdropping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Eavesdropping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Eavesdropping" title="Eavesdropping">Eavesdropping</a> is the act of surreptitiously listening to a private conversation, typically between hosts on a network. For instance, programs such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnivore_(FBI)" title="Carnivore (FBI)">Carnivore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Narus_(company)" title="Narus (company)">NarusInsight</a> have been used by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">FBI</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">NSA</a> to eavesdrop on the systems of <a href="/wiki/Internet_service_provider" title="Internet service provider">internet service providers</a>. Even machines that operate as a closed system (i.e., with no contact to the outside world) can be eavesdropped upon via monitoring the faint <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electro-magnetic</a> transmissions generated by the hardware; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TEMPEST" title="TEMPEST">TEMPEST</a> is a specification by the NSA referring to these attacks.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spoofing">Spoofing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Spoofing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Spoofing_attack" title="Spoofing attack">Spoofing</a>, in general, is a fraudulent or malicious practice in which communication is sent from an unknown source disguised as a source known to the receiver. Spoofing is most prevalent in communication mechanisms that lack a high level of security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tampering">Tampering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Tampering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tampering_(crime)" title="Tampering (crime)">Tampering</a> describes a malicious modification of products. So-called <a href="/wiki/Rootkit#bootkit" title="Rootkit">"Evil Maid" attacks</a> and security services planting of surveillance capability into routers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> are examples.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Privilege_escalation">Privilege escalation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Privilege escalation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Privilege_escalation" title="Privilege escalation">Privilege escalation</a> describes a situation where an attacker with some level of restricted access is able to, without authorization, elevate their privileges or access level. So for example a standard computer user may be able to fool the system into giving them access to restricted data; or even to "<a href="/wiki/Superuser" title="Superuser">become root</a>" and have full unrestricted access to a system.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phishing">Phishing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Phishing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Phishing" title="Phishing">Phishing</a> is the attempt to acquire sensitive information such as usernames, passwords, and credit card details directly from users.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Phishing is typically carried out by <a href="/wiki/Email_spoofing" title="Email spoofing">email spoofing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Instant_messaging" title="Instant messaging">instant messaging</a>, and it often directs users to enter details at a fake website whose look and feel are almost identical to the legitimate one. Preying on a victim's trusting, phishing can be classified as a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_engineering_(computer_security)" title="Social engineering (computer security)">social engineering</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clickjacking">Clickjacking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Clickjacking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Clickjacking" title="Clickjacking">Clickjacking</a>, also known as "UI redress attack" or "User Interface redress attack", is a malicious technique in which an attacker tricks a user into clicking on a button or link on another webpage while the user intended to click on the top level page. This is done using multiple transparent or opaque layers. The attacker is basically "<a href="/wiki/Page_hijacking" title="Page hijacking">hijacking</a>" the clicks meant for the top level page and routing them to some other irrelevant page, most likely owned by someone else. A similar technique can be used to hijack keystrokes. Carefully drafting a combination of stylesheets, iframes, buttons and text boxes, a user can be led into believing that they are typing the password or other information on some authentic webpage while it is being channeled into an invisible frame controlled by the attacker.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_engineering">Social engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Social engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_engineering_(security)" title="Social engineering (security)">Social engineering (security)</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Cryptographic_attacks" title="Category:Cryptographic attacks">Category:Cryptographic attacks</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_engineering_(computer_security)" title="Social engineering (computer security)">Social engineering</a> aims to convince a user to disclose secrets such as passwords, card numbers, etc. by, for example, impersonating a bank, a contractor, or a customer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>A popular and profitable cyber scam involves fake CEO emails sent to accounting and finance departments. In early 2016, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FBI" title="FBI">FBI</a> reported that the scam has cost US businesses more than $2bn in about two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Bucks" title="Milwaukee Bucks">Milwaukee Bucks</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NBA" title="NBA">NBA</a> team was the victim of this type of cyber scam with a perpetrator impersonating the team's president <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Peter_Feigin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Peter Feigin (page does not exist)">Peter Feigin</a>, resulting in the handover of all the team's employees' 2015 <a href="/wiki/Form_W-2" title="Form W-2">W-2</a> tax forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Systems_at_risk">Systems at risk</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Systems at risk">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Computer security is critical in almost any industry which uses computers. Currently, most electronic devices such as computers, laptops and cellphones come with built in firewall security software, but despite this, computers are not 100 percent accurate and dependable to protect our data (Smith, Grabosky &amp; Urbas, 2004.) There are many different ways of hacking into computers. It can be done through a network system, clicking into unknown links, connecting to unfamiliar Wi-Fi, downloading software and files from unsafe sites, power consumption, electromagnetic radiation waves, and many more. However, computers can be protected through well built software and hardware. By having strong internal interactions of properties, software complexity can prevent software crash and security failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAA_Computer_Security_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAA_Computer_Security-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_systems">Financial systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Financial systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Web sites and apps that accept or store <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Credit_card_number" title="Credit card number">credit card numbers</a>, brokerage accounts, and <a href="/wiki/Bank_account" title="Bank account">bank account</a> information are prominent hacking targets, because of the potential for immediate financial gain from transferring money, making purchases, or selling the information on the <a href="/wiki/Black_market" title="Black market">black market</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> In-store payment systems and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automated_teller_machine" title="Automated teller machine">ATMs</a> have also been tampered with in order to gather customer account data and <a href="/wiki/Personal_identification_number" title="Personal identification number">PINs</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Utilities_and_industrial_equipment">Utilities and industrial equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Utilities and industrial equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Computers control functions at many utilities, including coordination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telecommunications" title="Telecommunications">telecommunications</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_grid" title="Power grid">power grid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plants</a>, and valve opening and closing in water and gas networks. The Internet is a potential attack vector for such machines if connected, but the <a href="/wiki/Stuxnet" title="Stuxnet">Stuxnet</a> worm demonstrated that even equipment controlled by computers not connected to the Internet can be vulnerable to physical damage caused by malicious commands sent to industrial equipment (in that case <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium_enrichment" title="Uranium enrichment">uranium enrichment</a> centrifuges) which are infected via <a href="/wiki/Removable_media" title="Removable media">removable media</a>. In 2014, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_Emergency_Readiness_Team" title="Computer Emergency Readiness Team">Computer Emergency Readiness Team</a>, a division of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_Homeland_Security" title="Department of Homeland Security">Department of Homeland Security</a>, investigated 79 hacking incidents at energy companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Vulnerabilities in <a href="/wiki/Smart_meter" title="Smart meter">smart meters</a> (many of which use local radio or cellular communications) can cause problems with billing fraud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aviation">Aviation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Aviation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">aviation</a> industry is very reliant on a series of complex system which could be attacked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Computer_Security_in_Aviation_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Computer_Security_in_Aviation-18">[18]</a></sup> A simple power outage at one airport can cause repercussions worldwide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> much of the system relies on radio transmissions which could be disrupted,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and controlling aircraft over oceans is especially dangerous because radar surveillance only extends 175 to 225 miles offshore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> There is also potential for attack from within an aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The consequences of a successful attack range from loss of confidentiality to loss of system integrity, which may lead to more serious concerns such as exfiltration of data, network and <a href="/wiki/Air_traffic_control" title="Air traffic control">air traffic control</a> outages, which in turn can lead to airport closures, loss of aircraft, loss of passenger life, <a href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_World_Trade_Center" title="Collapse of the World Trade Center">damages on the ground</a> and to transportation infrastructure. A successful attack on a <a href="/wiki/Military_aviation" title="Military aviation">military aviation</a> system that controls <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Munitions" title="Munitions">munitions</a> could have even more serious consequences.</p>
<p>Europe has started to move towards centralised aviation network PENS (<a href="/wiki/Pan-European_Network_Service" title="Pan-European Network Service">Pan-European Network Service</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> which do provide a common IP-based network service across the European region covering voice and data communication and later on even more with NewPENS,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> similar USA has in NextGen program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumer_devices">Consumer devices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Consumer devices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Desktop computers and laptops are commonly infected with malware either to gather passwords or financial account information, or to construct a <a href="/wiki/Botnet" title="Botnet">botnet</a> to attack another target. <a href="/wiki/Samsung_Galaxy_Note_3" title="Samsung Galaxy Note 3">Smart phones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tablet_computer" title="Tablet computer">tablet computers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smart_watch" title="Smart watch">smart watches</a>, and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobile_devices" title="Mobile devices">mobile devices</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Quantified_Self" title="Quantified Self">Quantified Self</a> devices like <a href="/wiki/Activity_tracker" title="Activity tracker">activity trackers</a> have also become targets and many of these have sensors such as cameras, microphones, GPS receivers, compasses, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerometers" title="Accelerometers">accelerometers</a> which could be exploited, and may collect personal information, including sensitive health information. Wifi, Bluetooth, and cell phone networks on any of these devices could be used as attack vectors, and sensors might be remotely activated after a successful breach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nestwatch_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-nestwatch-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Home_automation" title="Home automation">Home automation</a> devices such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nest_thermostat" title="Nest thermostat">Nest thermostat</a> are also potential targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nestwatch_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-nestwatch-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Large_corporations">Large corporations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Large corporations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Large corporations are common targets. In many cases this is aimed at financial gain through identity theft and involves <a href="/wiki/Data_breach" title="Data breach">data breaches</a> such as the loss of millions of clients' credit card details by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Home_Depot" title="Home Depot">Home Depot</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Staples_Inc." title="Staples Inc.">Staples</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Target_Corporation" title="Target Corporation">Target Corporation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-milwaukee_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-milwaukee-29">[29]</a></sup> Medical records have been targeted for use in general identify theft, health insurance fraud, and impersonating patients to obtain prescription drugs for recreational purposes or resale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Not all attacks are financially motivated however; for example security firm <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HBGary_Federal" title="HBGary Federal">HBGary Federal</a> suffered a serious series of attacks in 2011 from hacktivist group <a href="/wiki/Anonymous_(group)" title="Anonymous (group)">Anonymous</a> in retaliation for the firm's CEO claiming to have infiltrated their group, <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures" title="Sony Pictures">Sony Pictures</a> <a href="/wiki/Sony_Pictures_Entertainment_hack" title="Sony Pictures Entertainment hack">was attacked in 2014</a> where the motive appears to have been to embarrass with data leaks, and cripple the company by wiping workstations and servers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Automobiles">Automobiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Automobiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>If access is gained to a car's internal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Controller_area_network" title="Controller area network">controller area network</a>, it is possible to disable the brakes and turn the steering wheel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vox_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-vox-35">[35]</a></sup> Computerized engine timing, <a href="/wiki/Cruise_control" title="Cruise control">cruise control</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-lock_brakes" title="Anti-lock brakes">anti-lock brakes</a>, seat belt tensioners, door locks, <a href="/wiki/Airbag" title="Airbag">airbags</a> and <a href="/wiki/Advanced_driver_assistance_systems" title="Advanced driver assistance systems">advanced driver assistance systems</a> make these disruptions possible, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-driving_car" title="Self-driving car">self-driving cars</a> go even further. <a href="/wiki/Connected_car" title="Connected car">Connected cars</a> may use wifi and bluetooth to communicate with onboard consumer devices, and the cell phone network to contact concierge and emergency assistance services or get navigational or entertainment information; each of these networks is a potential entry point for malware or an attacker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vox_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-vox-35">[35]</a></sup> Researchers in 2011 were even able to use a malicious <a href="/wiki/Compact_disc" title="Compact disc">compact disc</a> in a car's stereo system as a successful attack vector,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and cars with built-in voice recognition or remote assistance features have onboard microphones which could be used for eavesdropping.</p>
<p>A 2015 report by U.S. Senator Edward Markey criticized manufacturers' security measures as inadequate, and also highlighted privacy concerns about driving, location, and diagnostic data being collected, which is vulnerable to abuse by both manufacturers and hackers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Government and <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> computer systems are commonly attacked by activists<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tel_profile_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tel_profile-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcprofile_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcprofile-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-publications1_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-publications1-41">[41]</a></sup> and foreign powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-npr-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Local and regional government infrastructure such as <a href="/wiki/Traffic_light" title="Traffic light">traffic light</a> controls, police and intelligence agency communications, <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Personnel_Management_data_breach" title="Office of Personnel Management data breach">personnel records</a>, student records,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and financial systems are also potential targets as they are now all largely computerized. <a href="/wiki/Passport" title="Passport">Passports</a> and government <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ID_card" title="ID card">ID cards</a> that control access to facilities which use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RFID" title="RFID">RFID</a> can be vulnerable to <a href="/wiki/Phone_cloning" title="Phone cloning">cloning</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internet_of_Things_and_physical_vulnerabilities">Internet of Things and physical vulnerabilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Internet of Things and physical vulnerabilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internet_of_Things" title="Internet of Things">Internet of Things</a> (IoT) is the network of physical objects such as devices, vehicles, and buildings that are <a href="/wiki/Embedded_system" title="Embedded system">embedded</a> with <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sensor" title="Sensor">sensors</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Internet_access" title="Internet access">network connectivity</a> that enables them to collect and exchange data<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> - and concerns have been raised that this is being developed without appropriate consideration of the security challenges involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the IoT creates opportunities for more direct integration of the physical world into computer-based systems,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> it also provides opportunities for misuse. In particular, as the Internet of Things spreads widely, cyber attacks are likely to become an increasingly physical (rather than simply virtual) threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> If a front door's lock is connected to the Internet, and can be locked/unlocked from a phone, then a criminal could enter the home at the press of a button from a stolen or hacked phone. People could stand to lose much more than their credit card numbers in a world controlled by IoT-enabled devices. Thieves have also used electronic means to circumvent non-Internet-connected hotel door locks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_devices" title="Medical devices">Medical devices</a> have either been successfully attacked or had potentially deadly vulnerabilities demonstrated, including both in-hospital diagnostic equipment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and implanted devices including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacemaker" title="Pacemaker">pacemakers</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Insulin_pump" title="Insulin pump">insulin pumps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_security_breaches">Impact of security breaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Impact of security breaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Serious financial damage has been caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Security_breaches" title="Security breaches">security breaches</a>, but because there is no standard model for estimating the cost of an incident, the only data available is that which is made public by the organizations involved. "Several computer security consulting firms produce estimates of total worldwide losses attributable to <a href="/wiki/Computer_virus" title="Computer virus">virus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computer_worm" title="Computer worm">worm</a> attacks and to hostile digital acts in general. The 2003 loss estimates by these firms range from $13 billion (worms and viruses only) to $226 billion (for all forms of covert attacks). The reliability of these estimates is often challenged; the underlying methodology is basically anecdotal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, reasonable estimates of the financial cost of security breaches can actually help organizations make rational investment decisions. According to the classic <a href="/wiki/Gordon-Loeb_Model" title="Gordon-Loeb Model">Gordon-Loeb Model</a> analyzing the optimal investment level in information security, one can conclude that the amount a firm spends to protect information should generally be only a small fraction of the expected loss (i.e., the <a href="/wiki/Expected_value" title="Expected value">expected value</a> of the loss resulting from a cyber/information <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Security_breach" title="Security breach">security breach</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Attacker_motivation">Attacker motivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Attacker motivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As with <a href="/wiki/Physical_security" title="Physical security">physical security</a>, the motivations for breaches of computer security vary between attackers. Some are thrill-seekers or <a href="/wiki/Vandalism" title="Vandalism">vandals</a>, others are activists or criminals looking for financial gain. State-sponsored attackers are now common and well resourced, but started with amateurs such as <a href="/wiki/Markus_Hess" title="Markus Hess">Markus Hess</a> who hacked for the <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a>, as recounted by <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Stoll" title="Clifford Stoll">Clifford Stoll</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cuckoo%27s_Egg" title="The Cuckoo's Egg">The Cuckoo's Egg</a></i>.</p>
<p>A standard part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Threat_modelling" title="Threat modelling">threat modelling</a> for any particular system is to identify what might motivate an attack on that system, and who might be motivated to breach it. The level and detail of precautions will vary depending on the system to be secured. A home <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal computer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bank" title="Bank">bank</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Classified_information" title="Classified information">classified</a> military <a href="/wiki/Computer_network" title="Computer network">network</a> face very different threats, even when the underlying technologies in use are similar.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer_protection_.28countermeasures.29">Computer protection (countermeasures)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Computer protection (countermeasures)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In computer security a countermeasure is an action, device, procedure, or technique that reduces a <a href="/wiki/Threat_(computer)" title="Threat (computer)">threat</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_(computing)" title="Vulnerability (computing)">vulnerability</a>, or an <a href="/wiki/Attack_(computing)" title="Attack (computing)">attack</a> by eliminating or preventing it, by minimizing the harm it can cause, or by discovering and reporting it so that corrective action can be taken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rfc2828_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-rfc2828-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CNSSI4009_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-CNSSI4009-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some common countermeasures are listed in the following sections:</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Security_by_design">Security by design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Security by design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Secure_by_design" title="Secure by design">Secure by design</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Secure_by_design" title="Secure by design">Security by design</a>, or alternately secure by design, means that the software has been designed from the ground up to be secure. In this case, security is considered as a main feature.</p>
<p>Some of the techniques in this approach include:</p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_least_privilege" title="Principle of least privilege">principle of least privilege</a>, where each part of the system has only the privileges that are needed for its function. That way even if an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hacker_(computer_security)" title="Hacker (computer security)">attacker</a> gains access to that part, they have only limited access to the whole system.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Automated_theorem_proving" title="Automated theorem proving">Automated theorem proving</a> to prove the correctness of crucial software subsystems.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Code_review" title="Code review">Code reviews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unit_testing" title="Unit testing">unit testing</a>, approaches to make modules more secure where formal correctness proofs are not possible.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Defense_in_depth_(computing)" title="Defense in depth (computing)">Defense in depth</a>, where the design is such that more than one subsystem needs to be violated to compromise the integrity of the system and the information it holds.</li>
<li>Default secure settings, and design to "fail secure" rather than "fail insecure" (see <a href="/wiki/Fail-safe" title="Fail-safe">fail-safe</a> for the equivalent in <a href="/wiki/Safety_engineering" title="Safety engineering">safety engineering</a>). Ideally, a secure system should require a deliberate, conscious, knowledgeable and free decision on the part of legitimate authorities in order to make it insecure.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Audit_trail" title="Audit trail">Audit trails</a> tracking system activity, so that when a security breach occurs, the mechanism and extent of the breach can be determined. Storing audit trails remotely, where they can only be appended to, can keep intruders from covering their tracks.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Full_disclosure_(computer_security)" title="Full disclosure (computer security)">Full disclosure</a> of all vulnerabilities, to ensure that the "<a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_(computing)" title="Vulnerability (computing)">window of vulnerability</a>" is kept as short as possible when bugs are discovered.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Security_architecture">Security architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Security architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Open Security Architecture organization defines IT security architecture as "the design <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(software_development)" title="Artifact (software development)">artifacts</a> that describe how the security controls (security countermeasures) are positioned, and how they relate to the overall information technology architecture. These controls serve the purpose to maintain the system's quality attributes: confidentiality, integrity, availability, accountability and <a href="/wiki/Assurance_services" title="Assurance services">assurance services</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Techopedia defines security architecture as "a unified security design that addresses the necessities and potential risks involved in a certain scenario or environment. It also specifies when and where to apply security controls. The design process is generally reproducible." The key attributes of security architecture are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>the relationship of different components and how they depend on each other.</li>
<li>the determination of controls based on risk assessment, good practice, finances, and legal matters.</li>
<li>the standardization of controls.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Security_measures">Security measures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Security measures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A state of computer "security" is the conceptual ideal, attained by the use of the three processes: threat prevention, detection, and response. These processes are based on various policies and system components, which include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/User_account" title="User account">User account</a> <a href="/wiki/Access_control" title="Access control">access controls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cryptography" title="Cryptography">cryptography</a> can protect systems files and data, respectively.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firewall_(networking)" title="Firewall (networking)">Firewalls</a> are by far the most common prevention systems from a network security perspective as they can (if properly configured) shield access to internal network services, and block certain kinds of attacks through packet filtering. Firewalls can be both hardware- or software-based.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intrusion_Detection_System" title="Intrusion Detection System">Intrusion Detection System</a> (IDS) products are designed to detect network attacks in-progress and assist in post-attack <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forensics" title="Forensics">forensics</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Audit_trail" title="Audit trail">audit trails</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_logging" title="Data logging">logs</a> serve a similar function for individual systems.</li>
<li>"Response" is necessarily defined by the assessed security requirements of an individual system and may cover the range from simple upgrade of protections to notification of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal" title="Legal">legal</a> authorities, counter-attacks, and the like. In some special cases, a complete destruction of the compromised system is favored, as it may happen that not all the compromised resources are detected.</li>
</ul>
<p>Today, computer security comprises mainly "preventive" measures, like firewalls or an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exit_procedure" title="Exit procedure">exit procedure</a>. A firewall can be defined as a way of filtering network data between a host or a network and another network, such as the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>, and can be implemented as software running on the machine, hooking into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Network_stack" title="Network stack">network stack</a> (or, in the case of most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNIX" title="UNIX">UNIX</a>-based operating systems such as <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a>, built into the operating system <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kernel_(computer_science)" title="Kernel (computer science)">kernel</a>) to provide real time filtering and blocking. Another implementation is a so-called "physical firewall", which consists of a separate machine filtering network traffic. Firewalls are common amongst machines that are permanently connected to the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>.</p>
<p>Some organizations are turning to <a href="/wiki/Big_data" title="Big data">big data</a> platforms, such as <a href="/wiki/Apache_Hadoop" title="Apache Hadoop">Apache Hadoop</a>, to extend data accessibility and <a href="/wiki/Machine_learning" title="Machine learning">machine learning</a> to detect <a href="/wiki/Advanced_persistent_threat" title="Advanced persistent threat">advanced persistent threats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, relatively few organisations maintain computer systems with effective detection systems, and fewer still have organised response mechanisms in place. As result, as Reuters points out: "Companies for the first time report they are losing more through electronic theft of data than physical stealing of assets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The primary obstacle to effective eradication of cyber crime could be traced to excessive reliance on firewalls and other automated "detection" systems. Yet it is basic evidence gathering by using <a href="/wiki/Packet_capture_appliance" title="Packet capture appliance">packet capture appliances</a> that puts criminals behind bars.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vulnerability_management">Vulnerability management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Vulnerability management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_management" title="Vulnerability management">Vulnerability management</a></div>
<p>Vulnerability management is the cycle of identifying, and remediating or mitigating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Software_vulnerability" title="Software vulnerability">vulnerabilities</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> especially in <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a> and <a href="/wiki/Firmware" title="Firmware">firmware</a>. Vulnerability management is integral to computer security and <a href="/wiki/Network_security" title="Network security">network security</a>.</p>
<p>Vulnerabilities can be discovered with a <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_scanner" title="Vulnerability scanner">vulnerability scanner</a>, which analyzes a computer system in search of known vulnerabilities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Codenomicon_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Codenomicon-68">[68]</a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Open_port" title="Open port">open ports</a>, insecure software configuration, and susceptibility to <a href="/wiki/Malware" title="Malware">malware</a></p>
<p>Beyond vulnerability scanning, many organisations contract outside security auditors to run regular <a href="/wiki/Penetration_test" title="Penetration test">penetration tests</a> against their systems to identify vulnerabilities. In some sectors this is a contractual requirement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reducing_vulnerabilities">Reducing vulnerabilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Reducing vulnerabilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While <a href="/wiki/Formal_verification" title="Formal verification">formal verification</a> of the correctness of computer systems is possible,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> it is not yet common. Operating systems formally verified include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SeL4" title="SeL4">seL4</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/SYSGO" title="SYSGO">SYSGO</a>'s <a href="/wiki/PikeOS" title="PikeOS">PikeOS</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> – but these make up a very small percentage of the market.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cryptography" title="Cryptography">Cryptography</a> properly implemented is now virtually impossible to directly break. Breaking them requires some non-cryptographic input, such as a stolen key, stolen plaintext (at either end of the transmission), or some other extra cryptanalytic information.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two_factor_authentication" title="Two factor authentication">Two factor authentication</a> is a method for mitigating unauthorized access to a system or sensitive information. It requires "something you know"; a password or PIN, and "something you have"; a card, dongle, cellphone, or other piece of hardware. This increases security as an unauthorized person needs both of these to gain access.</p>
<p>Social engineering and direct computer access (physical) attacks can only be prevented by non-computer means, which can be difficult to enforce, relative to the sensitivity of the information. Training is often involved to help mitigate this risk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ALS14_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-ALS14-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SGA14_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-SGA14-76">[76]</a></sup> but even in a highly disciplined environments (e.g. military organizations), social engineering attacks can still be difficult to foresee and prevent.</p>
<p>It is possible to reduce an attacker's chances by keeping systems up to date with security patches and updates, using a security scanner or/and hiring competent people responsible for security. The effects of data loss/damage can be reduced by careful <a href="/wiki/Backup" title="Backup">backing up</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insurance" title="Insurance">insurance</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hardware_protection_mechanisms">Hardware protection mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Hardware protection mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Computer_security_compromised_by_hardware_failure" title="Computer security compromised by hardware failure">Computer security compromised by hardware failure</a></div>
<p>While hardware may be a source of insecurity, such as with microchip vulnerabilities maliciously introduced during the manufacturing process,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> hardware-based or assisted computer security also offers an alternative to software-only computer security. Using devices and methods such as <a href="/wiki/Dongle" title="Dongle">dongles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trusted_platform_module" title="Trusted platform module">trusted platform modules</a>, intrusion-aware cases, drive locks, disabling USB ports, and mobile-enabled access may be considered more secure due to the physical access (or sophisticated <a href="/wiki/Backdoor_(computing)" title="Backdoor (computing)">backdoor access</a>) required in order to be compromised. Each of these is covered in more detail below.</p>
<ul>
<li>USB <a href="/wiki/Dongle" title="Dongle">dongles</a> are typically used in software licensing schemes to unlock software capabilities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> but they can also be seen as a way to prevent unauthorized access to a computer or other device's software. The dongle, or key, essentially creates a secure encrypted tunnel between the software application and the key. The principle is that an encryption scheme on the dongle, such as <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Encryption_Standard" title="Advanced Encryption Standard">Advanced Encryption Standard</a> (AES) provides a stronger measure of security, since it is harder to hack and replicate the dongle than to simply copy the native software to another machine and use it. Another security application for dongles is to use them for accessing web-based content such as cloud software or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virtual_Private_Networks" title="Virtual Private Networks">Virtual Private Networks</a> (VPNs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In addition, a USB dongle can be configured to lock or unlock a computer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trusted_platform_module" title="Trusted platform module">Trusted platform modules</a> (TPMs) secure devices by integrating cryptographic capabilities onto access devices, through the use of microprocessors, or so-called computers-on-a-chip. TPMs used in conjunction with server-side software offer a way to detect and authenticate hardware devices, preventing unauthorized network and data access.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Computer_case#Intrusion_detection" title="Computer case">Computer case intrusion detection</a> refers to a push-button switch which is triggered when a computer case is opened. The firmware or BIOS is programmed to show an alert to the operator when the computer is booted up the next time.</li>
<li>Drive locks are essentially software tools to encrypt hard drives, making them inaccessible to thieves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Tools exist specifically for encrypting external drives as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></li>
<li>Disabling USB ports is a security option for preventing unauthorized and malicious access to an otherwise secure computer. Infected USB dongles connected to a network from a computer inside the firewall are considered by the magazine Network World as the most common hardware threat facing computer networks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></li>
<li>Mobile-enabled access devices are growing in popularity due to the ubiquitous nature of cell phones. Built-in capabilities such as <a href="/wiki/Bluetooth" title="Bluetooth">Bluetooth</a>, the newer <a href="/wiki/Bluetooth_low_energy" title="Bluetooth low energy">Bluetooth low energy</a> (LE), <a href="/wiki/Near_field_communication" title="Near field communication">Near field communication</a> (NFC) on non-iOS devices and <a href="/wiki/Biometrics" title="Biometrics">biometric</a> validation such as thumb print readers, as well as <a href="/wiki/QR_code" title="QR code">QR code</a> reader software designed for mobile devices, offer new, secure ways for mobile phones to connect to access control systems. These control systems provide computer security and can also be used for controlling access to secure buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secure_operating_systems">Secure operating systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Secure operating systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Security-evaluated_operating_system" title="Security-evaluated operating system">Security-evaluated operating system</a></div>
<p>One use of the term "computer security" refers to technology that is used to implement secure <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a>. In the 1980s the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a> (DoD) used the <a href="/wiki/Trusted_Computer_System_Evaluation_Criteria" title="Trusted Computer System Evaluation Criteria">"Orange Book"</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> standards, but the current international standard ISO/IEC 15408, "<a href="/wiki/Common_Criteria" title="Common Criteria">Common Criteria</a>" defines a number of progressively more stringent <a href="/wiki/Evaluation_Assurance_Level" title="Evaluation Assurance Level">Evaluation Assurance Levels</a>. Many common operating systems meet the EAL4 standard of being "Methodically Designed, Tested and Reviewed", but the <a href="/wiki/Formal_verification" title="Formal verification">formal verification</a> required for the highest levels means that they are uncommon. An example of an EAL6 ("Semiformally Verified Design and Tested") system is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/INTEGRITY-178B" title="INTEGRITY-178B">Integrity-178B</a>, which is used in the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A380" title="Airbus A380">Airbus A380</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darkreading_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-darkreading-88">[88]</a></sup> and several military jets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VITA_Technologies_Magazine_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-VITA_Technologies_Magazine-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secure_coding">Secure coding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Secure coding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Secure_coding" title="Secure coding">Secure coding</a></div>
<p>In software engineering, <a href="/wiki/Secure_coding" title="Secure coding">secure coding</a> aims to guard against the accidental introduction of security vulnerabilities. It is also possible to create software designed from the ground up to be secure. Such systems are "<a href="/wiki/Secure_by_design" title="Secure by design">secure by design</a>". Beyond this, <a href="/wiki/Formal_verification" title="Formal verification">formal verification</a> aims to prove the <a href="/wiki/Correctness_(computer_science)" title="Correctness (computer science)">correctness</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algorithms" title="Algorithms">algorithms</a> underlying a system;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> important for <a href="/wiki/Cryptographic_protocol" title="Cryptographic protocol">cryptographic protocols</a> for example.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capabilities_and_access_control_lists">Capabilities and access control lists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Capabilities and access control lists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Access_control_list" title="Access control list">Access control list</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capability_(computers)" title="Capability (computers)">Capability (computers)</a></div>
<p>Within computer systems, two of many <a href="/wiki/Computer_security_model" title="Computer security model">security models</a> capable of enforcing privilege separation are <a href="/wiki/Access_control_list" title="Access control list">access control lists</a> (ACLs) and <a href="/wiki/Capability-based_security" title="Capability-based security">capability-based security</a>. Using ACLs to confine programs has been proven to be insecure in many situations, such as if the host computer can be tricked into indirectly allowing restricted file access, an issue known as the <a href="/wiki/Confused_deputy_problem" title="Confused deputy problem">confused deputy problem</a>. It has also been shown that the promise of ACLs of giving access to an object to only one person can never be guaranteed in practice. Both of these problems are resolved by capabilities. This does not mean practical flaws exist in all ACL-based systems, but only that the designers of certain utilities must take responsibility to ensure that they do not introduce flaws.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Capabilities have been mostly restricted to research <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a>, while commercial OSs still use ACLs. Capabilities can, however, also be implemented at the language level, leading to a style of programming that is essentially a refinement of standard object-oriented design. An open source project in the area is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E_programming_language" title="E programming language">E language</a>.</p>
<p>The most secure computers are those not connected to the Internet and shielded from any interference. In the real world, the most secure systems are <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a> where <a href="/wiki/Security" title="Security">security</a> is not an add-on.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Response_to_breaches">Response to breaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Response to breaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Responding forcefully to attempted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Security_breaches" title="Security breaches">security breaches</a> (in the manner that one would for attempted physical security breaches) is often very difficult for a variety of reasons:</p>
<ul>
<li>Identifying attackers is difficult, as they are often in a different <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction" title="Jurisdiction">jurisdiction</a> to the systems they attempt to breach, and operate through proxies, temporary anonymous dial-up accounts, wireless connections, and other anonymising procedures which make backtracing difficult and are often located in yet another jurisdiction. If they successfully breach security, they are often able to delete <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_logging" title="Data logging">logs</a> to cover their tracks.</li>
<li>The sheer number of attempted attacks is so large that organisations cannot spend time pursuing each attacker (a typical home user with a permanent (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Cable_modem" title="Cable modem">cable modem</a>) connection will be attacked at least several times per day, so more attractive targets could be presumed to see many more). Note however, that most of the sheer bulk of these attacks are made by automated <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_scanner" title="Vulnerability scanner">vulnerability scanners</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computer_worm" title="Computer worm">computer worms</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement_officer" title="Law enforcement officer">Law enforcement officers</a> are often unfamiliar with <a href="/wiki/Information_technology" title="Information technology">information technology</a>, and so lack the skills and interest in pursuing attackers. There are also budgetary constraints. It has been argued that the high cost of technology, such as <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> testing, and improved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forensics" title="Forensics">forensics</a> mean less money for other kinds of law enforcement, so the overall rate of criminals not getting dealt with goes up as the cost of the technology increases. In addition, the identification of attackers across a network may require logs from various points in the network and in many countries, the release of these records to law enforcement (with the exception of being voluntarily surrendered by a <a href="/wiki/Network_administrator" title="Network administrator">network administrator</a> or a <a href="/wiki/System_administrator" title="System administrator">system administrator</a>) requires a <a href="/wiki/Search_warrant" title="Search warrant">search warrant</a> and, depending on the circumstances, the legal proceedings required can be drawn out to the point where the records are either regularly destroyed, or the information is no longer relevant.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_attacks_and_breaches">Notable attacks and breaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Notable attacks and breaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cyber-attacks" title="List of cyber-attacks">List of cyber-attacks</a></div>
<p>Some illustrative examples of different types of computer security breaches are given below.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Robert_Morris_and_the_first_computer_worm">Robert Morris and the first computer worm</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Robert Morris and the first computer worm">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Morris_worm" title="Morris worm">Morris worm</a></div>
<p>In 1988, only 60,000 computers were connected to the Internet, and most were mainframes, minicomputers and professional workstations. On November 2, 1988, many started to slow down, because they were running a malicious code that demanded processor time and that spread itself to other computers – the first internet "<a href="/wiki/Computer_worm" title="Computer worm">computer worm</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multiple_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-multiple-91">[91]</a></sup> The software was traced back to 23-year-old <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a> graduate student <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Tappan_Morris,_Jr." title="Robert Tappan Morris, Jr.">Robert Tappan Morris, Jr.</a> who said 'he wanted to count how many machines were connected to the Internet'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multiple_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-multiple-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rome_Laboratory">Rome Laboratory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Rome Laboratory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1994, over a hundred intrusions were made by unidentified crackers into the <a href="/wiki/Rome_Laboratory" title="Rome Laboratory">Rome Laboratory</a>, the US Air Force's main command and research facility. Using <a href="/wiki/Trojan_horse_(computing)" title="Trojan horse (computing)">trojan horses</a>, hackers were able to obtain unrestricted access to Rome's networking systems and remove traces of their activities. The intruders were able to obtain classified files, such as air tasking order systems data and furthermore able to penetrate connected networks of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration" title="National Aeronautics and Space Administration">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a>'s Goddard Space Flight Center, Wright-Patterson Air Force Base, some Defense contractors, and other private sector organizations, by posing as a trusted Rome center user.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="TJX_customer_credit_card_details">TJX customer credit card details</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: TJX customer credit card details">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In early 2007, American apparel and home goods company <a href="/wiki/TJX_Companies" title="TJX Companies">TJX</a> announced that it was the victim of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hacker_(computer_security)" title="Hacker (computer security)">unauthorized computer systems intrusion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> and that the hackers had accessed a system that stored data on <a href="/wiki/Credit_card" title="Credit card">credit card</a>, <a href="/wiki/Debit_card" title="Debit card">debit card</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheque" title="Cheque">check</a>, and merchandise return transactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stuxnet_attack">Stuxnet attack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Stuxnet attack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The computer worm known as <a href="/wiki/Stuxnet" title="Stuxnet">Stuxnet</a> reportedly ruined almost one-fifth of Iran's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_centrifuge" title="Nuclear centrifuge">nuclear centrifuges</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> by disrupting industrial <a href="/wiki/Programmable_logic_controller" title="Programmable logic controller">programmable logic controllers</a> (PLCs) in a targeted attack generally believed to have been launched by Israel and the United States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-us-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> although neither has publicly acknowledged this.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_surveillance_disclosures">Global surveillance disclosures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Global surveillance disclosures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Global_surveillance_disclosures_(2013%E2%80%93present)" title="Global surveillance disclosures (2013–present)">Global surveillance disclosures (2013–present)</a></div>
<p>In early 2013, massive breaches of computer security by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSA" title="NSA">NSA</a> were revealed, including deliberately inserting a backdoor in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NIST" title="NIST">NIST</a> standard for encryption<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> and tapping the links between <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>'s data centres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> These were disclosed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSA" title="NSA">NSA</a> contractor <a href="/wiki/Edward_Snowden" title="Edward Snowden">Edward Snowden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ARDinterview_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-ARDinterview-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Target_and_Home_Depot_breaches">Target and Home Depot breaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Target and Home Depot breaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2013 and 2014, a <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a>/<a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukrainian</a> hacking ring known as "Rescator" broke into <a href="/wiki/Target_Corporation" title="Target Corporation">Target Corporation</a> computers in 2013, stealing roughly 40 million credit cards,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> and then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Home_Depot" title="Home Depot">Home Depot</a> computers in 2014, stealing between 53 and 56 million credit card numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Warnings were delivered at both corporations, but ignored; physical security breaches using <a href="/wiki/Self-checkout" title="Self-checkout">self checkout machines</a> are believed to have played a large role. "The malware utilized is absolutely unsophisticated and uninteresting," says Jim Walter, director of threat intelligence operations at security technology company McAfee – meaning that the heists could have easily been stopped by existing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antivirus" title="Antivirus">antivirus</a> software had administrators responded to the warnings. The size of the thefts has resulted in major attention from state and Federal United States authorities and the investigation is ongoing.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ashley_Madison_breach">Ashley Madison breach</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_security&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Ashley Madison breach">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ashley_Madison_data_breach" title="Ashley Madison data breach">Ashley Madison Data Breach</a></div>
<p>In July 2015, a hacker group known as "The Impact Team" successfully breached the extramarital relationship website Ashley Madison.
